<h2><span class="mw-headline" id="Legal_system">Legal system</span></h2>
<div role="note" class="hatnote">Further information: <a href="/wiki/European_Union_law" title="European Union law">European Union law</a>, <a href="/wiki/Treaties_of_the_European_Union" title="Treaties of the European Union">Treaties of the European Union</a>, and <a href="/wiki/Charter_of_Fundamental_Rights_of_the_European_Union" title="Charter of Fundamental Rights of the European Union">Charter of Fundamental Rights of the European Union</a></div>
<div class="thumb tright">
<div class="thumbinner" style="width:222px;"><a href="/wiki/File:EuGH_Saal_(gro%C3%9Fe_Kammer_mit_13_Richter).JPG" class="image"><img alt="" src="//upload.wikimedia.org/wikipedia/commons/thumb/4/4e/EuGH_Saal_%28gro%C3%9Fe_Kammer_mit_13_Richter%29.JPG/220px-EuGH_Saal_%28gro%C3%9Fe_Kammer_mit_13_Richter%29.JPG" width="220" height="120" class="thumbimage" srcset="//upload.wikimedia.org/wikipedia/commons/thumb/4/4e/EuGH_Saal_%28gro%C3%9Fe_Kammer_mit_13_Richter%29.JPG/330px-EuGH_Saal_%28gro%C3%9Fe_Kammer_mit_13_Richter%29.JPG 1.5x, //upload.wikimedia.org/wikipedia/commons/thumb/4/4e/EuGH_Saal_%28gro%C3%9Fe_Kammer_mit_13_Richter%29.JPG/440px-EuGH_Saal_%28gro%C3%9Fe_Kammer_mit_13_Richter%29.JPG 2x" data-file-width="1744" data-file-height="948" /></a>
<div class="thumbcaption">
<div class="magnify"><a href="/wiki/File:EuGH_Saal_(gro%C3%9Fe_Kammer_mit_13_Richter).JPG" class="internal" title="Enlarge"></a></div>
The <a href="/wiki/European_Court_of_Justice" title="European Court of Justice">Court of Justice</a>, seated in <a href="/wiki/Luxembourg" title="Luxembourg">Luxembourg</a>.</div>
</div>
</div>
<p>The EU is based on a series of <a href="/wiki/Treaties_of_the_European_Union" title="Treaties of the European Union">treaties</a>. These first established the European Community and the EU, and then made amendments to those founding treaties.<sup id="cite_ref-111" class="reference"><a href="#cite_note-111">[103]</a></sup> These are power-giving treaties which set broad policy goals and establish institutions with the necessary legal powers to implement those goals. These legal powers include the ability to enact legislation<sup id="cite_ref-art249_112-0" class="reference"><a href="#cite_note-art249-112">[i]</a></sup> which can directly affect all member states and their inhabitants.<sup id="cite_ref-Direct_Effect_113-0" class="reference"><a href="#cite_note-Direct_Effect-113">[j]</a></sup> The EU has <a href="/wiki/Legal_personality" title="Legal personality">legal personality</a>, with the right to sign agreements and international treaties.<sup id="cite_ref-114" class="reference"><a href="#cite_note-114">[104]</a></sup></p>
<p>Under the principle of <a href="/wiki/Supremacy_(European_Union_law)" class="mw-redirect" title="Supremacy (European Union law)">supremacy</a>, national courts are required to enforce the treaties that their member states have ratified, and thus the laws enacted under them, even if doing so requires them to ignore conflicting national law, and (within limits) even constitutional provisions.<sup id="cite_ref-Supremacy_115-0" class="reference"><a href="#cite_note-Supremacy-115">[k]</a></sup></p>
<h3><span class="mw-headline" id="Courts_of_Justice">Courts of Justice</span></h3>
<p>The <a href="/wiki/Judiciary" title="Judiciary">judicial branch</a> of the EU—formally called the <a href="/wiki/Court_of_Justice_of_the_European_Union" title="Court of Justice of the European Union">Court of Justice of the European Union</a>—consists of three courts: the <a href="/wiki/European_Court_of_Justice" title="European Court of Justice">Court of Justice</a>, the <a href="/wiki/General_Court_(European_Union)" title="General Court (European Union)">General Court</a>, and the <a href="/wiki/European_Union_Civil_Service_Tribunal" title="European Union Civil Service Tribunal">European Union Civil Service Tribunal</a>. Together they interpret and apply the treaties and the law of the EU.<sup id="cite_ref-116" class="reference"><a href="#cite_note-116">[105]</a></sup></p>
<p>The Court of Justice primarily deals with cases taken by member states, the institutions, and <a href="/wiki/Preliminary_ruling" title="Preliminary ruling">cases referred to it</a> by the courts of member states.<sup id="cite_ref-117" class="reference"><a href="#cite_note-117">[106]</a></sup> The General Court mainly deals with cases taken by individuals and companies directly before the EU's courts,<sup id="cite_ref-118" class="reference"><a href="#cite_note-118">[107]</a></sup> and the European Union Civil Service Tribunal adjudicates in disputes between the European Union and <a href="/wiki/European_Civil_Service" title="European Civil Service">its civil service</a>.<sup id="cite_ref-119" class="reference"><a href="#cite_note-119">[108]</a></sup> Decisions from the General Court can be appealed to the Court of Justice but only on a point of law.<sup id="cite_ref-120" class="reference"><a href="#cite_note-120">[109]</a></sup></p>
<h3><span class="mw-headline" id="Fundamental_rights">Fundamental rights</span></h3>
<div class="thumb tright">
<div class="thumbinner" style="width:222px;"><a href="/wiki/File:Remise_du_Prix_Sakharov_%C3%A0_Aung_San_Suu_Kyi_Strasbourg_22_octobre_2013-14.jpg" class="image"><img alt="" src="//upload.wikimedia.org/wikipedia/commons/thumb/a/a7/Remise_du_Prix_Sakharov_%C3%A0_Aung_San_Suu_Kyi_Strasbourg_22_octobre_2013-14.jpg/220px-Remise_du_Prix_Sakharov_%C3%A0_Aung_San_Suu_Kyi_Strasbourg_22_octobre_2013-14.jpg" width="220" height="147" class="thumbimage" srcset="//upload.wikimedia.org/wikipedia/commons/thumb/a/a7/Remise_du_Prix_Sakharov_%C3%A0_Aung_San_Suu_Kyi_Strasbourg_22_octobre_2013-14.jpg/330px-Remise_du_Prix_Sakharov_%C3%A0_Aung_San_Suu_Kyi_Strasbourg_22_octobre_2013-14.jpg 1.5x, //upload.wikimedia.org/wikipedia/commons/thumb/a/a7/Remise_du_Prix_Sakharov_%C3%A0_Aung_San_Suu_Kyi_Strasbourg_22_octobre_2013-14.jpg/440px-Remise_du_Prix_Sakharov_%C3%A0_Aung_San_Suu_Kyi_Strasbourg_22_octobre_2013-14.jpg 2x" data-file-width="5184" data-file-height="3456" /></a>
<div class="thumbcaption">
<div class="magnify"><a href="/wiki/File:Remise_du_Prix_Sakharov_%C3%A0_Aung_San_Suu_Kyi_Strasbourg_22_octobre_2013-14.jpg" class="internal" title="Enlarge"></a></div>
The ceremony of the 1990 <a href="/wiki/Sakharov_Prize" title="Sakharov Prize">Sakharov Prize</a> awarded to <a href="/wiki/Aung_San_Suu_Kyi" title="Aung San Suu Kyi">Aung San Suu Kyi</a> by <a href="/wiki/Martin_Schulz" title="Martin Schulz">Martin Schulz</a>, inside the Parliament's Strasbourg hemicycle, in 2013.</div>
</div>
</div>
<p>The treaties declare that the EU itself is "founded on the values of respect for <a href="/wiki/Human_dignity" class="mw-redirect" title="Human dignity">human dignity</a>, <a href="/wiki/Liberty" title="Liberty">freedom</a>, democracy, <a href="/wiki/Equality_before_the_law" title="Equality before the law">equality</a>, the <a href="/wiki/Rule_of_law" title="Rule of law">rule of law</a> and respect for human rights, including the rights of persons belonging to <a href="/wiki/Minority_group" title="Minority group">minorities</a>&#160;... in a society in which pluralism, non-discrimination, tolerance, justice, solidarity and equality between women and men prevail."<sup id="cite_ref-121" class="reference"><a href="#cite_note-121">[110]</a></sup></p>
<p>In 2009, the <a href="/wiki/Lisbon_Treaty" class="mw-redirect" title="Lisbon Treaty">Lisbon Treaty</a> gave legal effect to the <a href="/wiki/Charter_of_Fundamental_Rights_of_the_European_Union" title="Charter of Fundamental Rights of the European Union">Charter of Fundamental Rights of the European Union</a>. The charter is a codified catalogue of <a href="/wiki/Fundamental_right" class="mw-redirect" title="Fundamental right">fundamental rights</a> against which the EU's legal acts can be judged. It consolidates many rights which were previously recognised by the Court of Justice and derived from the "constitutional traditions common to the member states."<sup id="cite_ref-122" class="reference"><a href="#cite_note-122">[111]</a></sup> The Court of Justice has long recognised fundamental rights and has, on occasion, invalidated EU legislation based on its failure to adhere to those fundamental rights.<sup id="cite_ref-europarl-rights_123-0" class="reference"><a href="#cite_note-europarl-rights-123">[112]</a></sup></p>
<p>Although signing the <a href="/wiki/European_Convention_on_Human_Rights" title="European Convention on Human Rights">European Convention on Human Rights</a> (ECHR) is a condition for EU membership,<sup id="cite_ref-124" class="reference"><a href="#cite_note-124">[l]</a></sup> previously, the EU itself could not accede to the Convention as it is neither a state<sup id="cite_ref-125" class="reference"><a href="#cite_note-125">[m]</a></sup> nor had the competence to accede.<sup id="cite_ref-126" class="reference"><a href="#cite_note-126">[n]</a></sup> The Lisbon Treaty and Protocol 14 to the ECHR have changed this: the former binds the EU to accede to the Convention while the latter formally permits it.</p>
<p>Although, the EU is independent from Council of Europe, they share purpose and ideas especially on rule of law, human rights and democracy. Further <a href="/wiki/European_Convention_on_Human_Rights" title="European Convention on Human Rights">European Convention on Human Rights</a> and <a href="/wiki/European_Social_Charter" title="European Social Charter">European Social Charter</a>, the <a href="/wiki/Source_of_law" class="mw-redirect" title="Source of law">source of law</a> of <a href="/wiki/Charter_of_Fundamental_Rights_of_the_European_Union" title="Charter of Fundamental Rights of the European Union">Charter of Fundamental Rights</a> are created by Council of Europe. The EU also promoted human rights issues in the wider world. The EU opposes the death penalty and has proposed its worldwide abolition. Abolition of the death penalty is a condition for EU membership.<sup id="cite_ref-127" class="reference"><a href="#cite_note-127">[113]</a></sup></p>
<h3><span class="mw-headline" id="Acts">Acts</span></h3>
<p>The main legal acts of the EU come in three forms: <a href="/wiki/Regulation_(European_Union)" title="Regulation (European Union)">regulations</a>, <a href="/wiki/Directive_(European_Union)" title="Directive (European Union)">directives</a>, and <a href="/wiki/Decision_(European_Union)" title="Decision (European Union)">decisions</a>. Regulations become law in all member states the moment they come into force, without the requirement for any implementing measures,<sup id="cite_ref-128" class="reference"><a href="#cite_note-128">[o]</a></sup> and automatically override conflicting domestic provisions.<sup id="cite_ref-art249_112-1" class="reference"><a href="#cite_note-art249-112">[i]</a></sup> Directives require member states to achieve a certain result while leaving them discretion as to how to achieve the result. The details of how they are to be implemented are left to member states.<sup id="cite_ref-129" class="reference"><a href="#cite_note-129">[p]</a></sup> When the time limit for implementing directives passes, they may, under certain conditions, have <a href="/wiki/Direct_effect" class="mw-redirect" title="Direct effect">direct effect</a> in national law against member states.</p>
<p>Decisions offer an alternative to the two above modes of legislation. They are legal acts which only apply to specified individuals, companies or a particular member state. They are most often used in <a href="/wiki/Competition_law" title="Competition law">competition law</a>, or on rulings on State Aid, but are also frequently used for procedural or administrative matters within the institutions. Regulations, directives, and decisions are of equal legal value and apply without any formal hierarchy.<sup id="cite_ref-130" class="reference"><a href="#cite_note-130">[114]</a></sup></p>
<h3><span class="mw-headline" id="Area_of_freedom.2C_security_and_justice">Area of freedom, security and justice</span></h3>
<div role="note" class="hatnote">Further information: <a href="/wiki/Area_of_freedom,_security_and_justice" title="Area of freedom, security and justice">Area of freedom, security and justice</a></div>
<div class="thumb tright">
<div class="thumbinner" style="width:222px;"><a href="/wiki/File:OffeneGrenzeNiederndorf-Oberaudorf.jpg" class="image"><img alt="" src="//upload.wikimedia.org/wikipedia/commons/thumb/7/76/OffeneGrenzeNiederndorf-Oberaudorf.jpg/220px-OffeneGrenzeNiederndorf-Oberaudorf.jpg" width="220" height="165" class="thumbimage" srcset="//upload.wikimedia.org/wikipedia/commons/thumb/7/76/OffeneGrenzeNiederndorf-Oberaudorf.jpg/330px-OffeneGrenzeNiederndorf-Oberaudorf.jpg 1.5x, //upload.wikimedia.org/wikipedia/commons/thumb/7/76/OffeneGrenzeNiederndorf-Oberaudorf.jpg/440px-OffeneGrenzeNiederndorf-Oberaudorf.jpg 2x" data-file-width="600" data-file-height="450" /></a>
<div class="thumbcaption">
<div class="magnify"><a href="/wiki/File:OffeneGrenzeNiederndorf-Oberaudorf.jpg" class="internal" title="Enlarge"></a></div>
The borders inside the <a href="/wiki/Schengen_Area" title="Schengen Area">Schengen Area</a> between Germany and Austria</div>
</div>
</div>
<p>Since the creation of the EU in 1993, it has developed its competencies in the area of freedom, security and justice, initially at an intergovernmental level and later by supranationalism. To this end, agencies have been established that co-ordinate associated actions: <a href="/wiki/Europol" title="Europol">Europol</a> for co-operation of police forces,<sup id="cite_ref-131" class="reference"><a href="#cite_note-131">[115]</a></sup> <a href="/wiki/Eurojust" title="Eurojust">Eurojust</a> for co-operation between prosecutors,<sup id="cite_ref-132" class="reference"><a href="#cite_note-132">[116]</a></sup> and <a href="/wiki/Frontex" title="Frontex">Frontex</a> for co-operation between border control authorities.<sup id="cite_ref-133" class="reference"><a href="#cite_note-133">[117]</a></sup> The EU also operates the <a href="/wiki/Schengen_Information_System" title="Schengen Information System">Schengen Information System</a><sup id="cite_ref-Internal_borders_20-1" class="reference"><a href="#cite_note-Internal_borders-20">[15]</a></sup> which provides a common database for police and immigration authorities. This co-operation had to particularly be developed with the advent of open borders through the <a href="/wiki/Schengen_Agreement" title="Schengen Agreement">Schengen Agreement</a> and the associated cross border crime.</p>
<p>Furthermore, the Union has legislated in areas such as <a href="/wiki/European_Arrest_Warrant" title="European Arrest Warrant">extradition</a>,<sup id="cite_ref-134" class="reference"><a href="#cite_note-134">[118]</a></sup> family law,<sup id="cite_ref-135" class="reference"><a href="#cite_note-135">[119]</a></sup> asylum law,<sup id="cite_ref-136" class="reference"><a href="#cite_note-136">[120]</a></sup> and criminal justice.<sup id="cite_ref-137" class="reference"><a href="#cite_note-137">[121]</a></sup> Prohibitions against sexual and nationality discrimination have a long standing in the treaties.<sup id="cite_ref-art39.2F141_Rome_138-0" class="reference"><a href="#cite_note-art39.2F141_Rome-138">[q]</a></sup> In more recent years, these have been supplemented by powers to legislate against discrimination based on race, religion, disability, age, and sexual orientation.<sup id="cite_ref-art2.287.29_Amsterdam_139-0" class="reference"><a href="#cite_note-art2.287.29_Amsterdam-139">[r]</a></sup> By virtue of these powers, the EU has enacted legislation on <a href="/wiki/Sexism" title="Sexism">sexual discrimination</a> in the work-place, <a href="/wiki/Ageism" title="Ageism">age discrimination</a>, and <a href="/wiki/Racism" title="Racism">racial discrimination</a>.<sup id="cite_ref-140" class="reference"><a href="#cite_note-140">[s]</a></sup></p>
<h2><span class="mw-headline" id="Foreign_relations">Foreign relations</span></h2>
<div role="note" class="hatnote">Main articles: <a href="/wiki/Foreign_relations_of_the_European_Union" title="Foreign relations of the European Union">Foreign relations of the European Union</a>, <a href="/wiki/Common_Foreign_and_Security_Policy" title="Common Foreign and Security Policy">Common Foreign and Security Policy</a>, and <a href="/wiki/European_External_Action_Service" title="European External Action Service">European External Action Service</a></div>
<div class="thumb tright">
<div class="thumbinner" style="width:222px;"><a href="/wiki/File:2010_G-20_Seoul_summit.jpg" class="image"><img alt="" src="//upload.wikimedia.org/wikipedia/commons/thumb/c/c5/2010_G-20_Seoul_summit.jpg/220px-2010_G-20_Seoul_summit.jpg" width="220" height="139" class="thumbimage" srcset="//upload.wikimedia.org/wikipedia/commons/thumb/c/c5/2010_G-20_Seoul_summit.jpg/330px-2010_G-20_Seoul_summit.jpg 1.5x, //upload.wikimedia.org/wikipedia/commons/thumb/c/c5/2010_G-20_Seoul_summit.jpg/440px-2010_G-20_Seoul_summit.jpg 2x" data-file-width="2200" data-file-height="1394" /></a>
<div class="thumbcaption">
<div class="magnify"><a href="/wiki/File:2010_G-20_Seoul_summit.jpg" class="internal" title="Enlarge"></a></div>
The EU participates in all <a href="/wiki/G8" class="mw-redirect" title="G8">G8</a> and <a href="/wiki/G-20_major_economies" class="mw-redirect" title="G-20 major economies">G20</a> summits. (<a href="/wiki/2010_G-20_Seoul_summit" class="mw-redirect" title="2010 G-20 Seoul summit">G20 summit in Seoul</a>)</div>
</div>
</div>
<p>Foreign policy co-operation between member states dates from the establishment of the Community in 1957, when member states negotiated as a bloc in international trade negotiations under the <a href="/wiki/Common_commercial_policy" title="Common commercial policy">common commercial policy</a>.<sup id="cite_ref-141" class="reference"><a href="#cite_note-141">[122]</a></sup> Steps for a more wide-ranging co-ordination in foreign relations began in 1970 with the establishment of <a href="/wiki/European_Political_Cooperation" title="European Political Cooperation">European Political Cooperation</a> which created an informal consultation process between member states with the aim of forming common foreign policies. It was not, however, until 1987 when European Political Cooperation was introduced on a formal basis by the <a href="/wiki/Single_European_Act" title="Single European Act">Single European Act</a>. EPC was renamed as the <a href="/wiki/Common_Foreign_and_Security_Policy" title="Common Foreign and Security Policy">Common Foreign and Security Policy</a> (CFSP) by the <a href="/wiki/Maastricht_Treaty" title="Maastricht Treaty">Maastricht Treaty</a>.<sup id="cite_ref-EPC_142-0" class="reference"><a href="#cite_note-EPC-142">[123]</a></sup></p>
<div class="thumb tleft">
<div class="thumbinner" style="width:172px;"><a href="/wiki/File:Federica_Mogherini_Official.jpg" class="image"><img alt="" src="//upload.wikimedia.org/wikipedia/commons/thumb/5/52/Federica_Mogherini_Official.jpg/170px-Federica_Mogherini_Official.jpg" width="170" height="217" class="thumbimage" srcset="//upload.wikimedia.org/wikipedia/commons/thumb/5/52/Federica_Mogherini_Official.jpg/255px-Federica_Mogherini_Official.jpg 1.5x, //upload.wikimedia.org/wikipedia/commons/thumb/5/52/Federica_Mogherini_Official.jpg/340px-Federica_Mogherini_Official.jpg 2x" data-file-width="1731" data-file-height="2206" /></a>
<div class="thumbcaption">
<div class="magnify"><a href="/wiki/File:Federica_Mogherini_Official.jpg" class="internal" title="Enlarge"></a></div>
<a href="/wiki/High_Representative_of_the_Union_for_Foreign_Affairs_and_Security_Policy" title="High Representative of the Union for Foreign Affairs and Security Policy">High Representative of the Union for Foreign Affairs and Security Policy</a>, <a href="/wiki/Federica_Mogherini" title="Federica Mogherini">Federica Mogherini</a></div>
</div>
</div>
<p>The aims of the CFSP are to promote both the EU's own interests and those of the <a href="/wiki/International_community" title="International community">international community</a> as a whole, including the furtherance of international co-operation, respect for human rights, democracy, and the rule of law.<sup id="cite_ref-art11_Union_143-0" class="reference"><a href="#cite_note-art11_Union-143">[124]</a></sup> The CFSP requires unanimity among the member states on the appropriate policy to follow on any particular issue. The unanimity and difficult issues treated under the CFSP sometimes lead to disagreements, such as those which occurred over the <a href="/wiki/Iraq_War" title="Iraq War">war in Iraq</a>.<sup id="cite_ref-BBC_Iraq_statement_144-0" class="reference"><a href="#cite_note-BBC_Iraq_statement-144">[125]</a></sup></p>
<p>The coordinator and representative of the CFSP within the EU is the <a href="/wiki/High_Representative_of_the_Union_for_Foreign_Affairs_and_Security_Policy" title="High Representative of the Union for Foreign Affairs and Security Policy">High Representative of the Union for Foreign Affairs and Security Policy</a> who speaks on behalf of the EU in foreign policy and defence matters, and has the task of articulating the positions expressed by the member states on these fields of policy into a common alignment. The High Representative heads up the <a href="/wiki/European_External_Action_Service" title="European External Action Service">European External Action Service</a> (EEAS), a unique EU department<sup id="cite_ref-23Oct_145-0" class="reference"><a href="#cite_note-23Oct-145">[126]</a></sup> that has been officially implemented and operational since 1 December 2010 on the occasion of the first anniversary of the entry into force of the <a href="/wiki/Treaty_of_Lisbon" title="Treaty of Lisbon">Treaty of Lisbon</a>.<sup id="cite_ref-146" class="reference"><a href="#cite_note-146">[127]</a></sup> The EEAS will serve as a foreign ministry and <a href="/wiki/Diplomatic_corps" title="Diplomatic corps">diplomatic corps</a> for the European Union.<sup id="cite_ref-Europa_EEAS_147-0" class="reference"><a href="#cite_note-Europa_EEAS-147">[128]</a></sup></p>
<p>Besides the emerging international policy of the European Union, the international influence of the EU is also felt through <a href="/wiki/Enlargement_of_the_European_Union" title="Enlargement of the European Union">enlargement</a>. The perceived benefits of becoming a member of the EU act as an incentive for both political and economic reform in states wishing to fulfil the EU's accession criteria, and are considered an important factor contributing to the reform of European formerly Communist countries.<sup id="cite_ref-148" class="reference"><a href="#cite_note-148">[129]</a></sup><sup class="reference" style="white-space:nowrap;">:762</sup> This influence on the internal affairs of other countries is generally referred to as "<a href="/wiki/Soft_power" title="Soft power">soft power</a>", as opposed to military "hard power".<sup id="cite_ref-Soft_Power_149-0" class="reference"><a href="#cite_note-Soft_Power-149">[130]</a></sup></p>
<h3><span class="mw-headline" id="Military">Military</span></h3>
<div role="note" class="hatnote">Main article: <a href="/wiki/Military_of_the_European_Union" title="Military of the European Union">Military of the European Union</a></div>
<div class="thumb tright">
<div class="thumbinner" style="width:172px;"><a href="/wiki/File:Frontex_HQ_Warsaw_Spire_office_complex_Warsaw.jpg" class="image"><img alt="" src="//upload.wikimedia.org/wikipedia/commons/thumb/2/21/Frontex_HQ_Warsaw_Spire_office_complex_Warsaw.jpg/170px-Frontex_HQ_Warsaw_Spire_office_complex_Warsaw.jpg" width="170" height="225" class="thumbimage" srcset="//upload.wikimedia.org/wikipedia/commons/thumb/2/21/Frontex_HQ_Warsaw_Spire_office_complex_Warsaw.jpg/255px-Frontex_HQ_Warsaw_Spire_office_complex_Warsaw.jpg 1.5x, //upload.wikimedia.org/wikipedia/commons/thumb/2/21/Frontex_HQ_Warsaw_Spire_office_complex_Warsaw.jpg/340px-Frontex_HQ_Warsaw_Spire_office_complex_Warsaw.jpg 2x" data-file-width="3279" data-file-height="4339" /></a>
<div class="thumbcaption">
<div class="magnify"><a href="/wiki/File:Frontex_HQ_Warsaw_Spire_office_complex_Warsaw.jpg" class="internal" title="Enlarge"></a></div>
Seat of <a href="/wiki/Frontex" title="Frontex">Frontex</a> in <a href="/wiki/Warsaw" title="Warsaw">Warsaw</a></div>
</div>
</div>
<p>The predecessors of the European Union were not devised as a military alliance because <a href="/wiki/NATO" title="NATO">NATO</a> was largely seen as appropriate and sufficient for defence purposes.<sup id="cite_ref-FOOTNOTEWilkinson2007100_150-0" class="reference"><a href="#cite_note-FOOTNOTEWilkinson2007100-150">[131]</a></sup> 22 EU members are members of NATO<sup id="cite_ref-151" class="reference"><a href="#cite_note-151">[132]</a></sup> while the remaining member states follow policies of <a href="/wiki/Neutrality_(international_relations)" class="mw-redirect" title="Neutrality (international relations)">neutrality</a>.<sup id="cite_ref-152" class="reference"><a href="#cite_note-152">[133]</a></sup> The <a href="/wiki/Western_European_Union" title="Western European Union">Western European Union</a>, a military alliance with a mutual defence clause, was disbanded in 2010 as its role had been transferred to the EU.<sup id="cite_ref-153" class="reference"><a href="#cite_note-153">[134]</a></sup></p>
<div class="thumb tleft">
<div class="thumbinner" style="width:222px;"><a href="/wiki/File:A400M.jpg" class="image"><img alt="" src="//upload.wikimedia.org/wikipedia/commons/thumb/b/ba/A400M.jpg/220px-A400M.jpg" width="220" height="172" class="thumbimage" srcset="//upload.wikimedia.org/wikipedia/commons/thumb/b/ba/A400M.jpg/330px-A400M.jpg 1.5x, //upload.wikimedia.org/wikipedia/commons/thumb/b/ba/A400M.jpg/440px-A400M.jpg 2x" data-file-width="1996" data-file-height="1564" /></a>
<div class="thumbcaption">
<div class="magnify"><a href="/wiki/File:A400M.jpg" class="internal" title="Enlarge"></a></div>
An <a href="/wiki/Airbus_A400M_Atlas" title="Airbus A400M Atlas">A400M</a> <a href="/wiki/Military_transport_aircraft" title="Military transport aircraft">military transport aircraft</a> built by <a href="/wiki/Airbus_Group" title="Airbus Group">Airbus Group SE</a> (<i><a href="/wiki/Societas_Europaea" title="Societas Europaea">Societas Europaea</a></i>; <a href="/wiki/Latin_language" class="mw-redirect" title="Latin language">Latin</a>: European company)</div>
</div>
</div>
<p>According to the <a href="/wiki/Stockholm_International_Peace_Research_Institute" title="Stockholm International Peace Research Institute">Stockholm International Peace Research Institute</a> (SIPRI), the United Kingdom spent $61 billion on defence in 2014, placing it fifth in the world, while France spent $53 billion, the sixth largest.<sup id="cite_ref-154" class="reference"><a href="#cite_note-154">[135]</a></sup> Together, the UK and France account for approximately 40 per cent of European countries' defence budget and 50 per cent of their military capacity.<sup id="cite_ref-155" class="reference"><a href="#cite_note-155">[136]</a></sup> Both are officially recognised <a href="/wiki/Nuclear_weapons_state" class="mw-redirect" title="Nuclear weapons state">nuclear weapon states</a> holding permanent seats on the <a href="/wiki/United_Nations_Security_Council" title="United Nations Security Council">United Nations Security Council</a>.</p>
<p>Following the <a href="/wiki/Kosovo_War" title="Kosovo War">Kosovo War</a> in 1999, the <a href="/wiki/European_Council" title="European Council">European Council</a> agreed that "the Union must have the capacity for autonomous action, backed by credible military forces, the means to decide to use them, and the readiness to do so, in order to respond to international crises without prejudice to actions by NATO". To that end, a number of efforts were made to increase the EU's military capability, notably the <a href="/wiki/Helsinki_Headline_Goal" title="Helsinki Headline Goal">Helsinki Headline Goal</a> process. After much discussion, the most concrete result was the <a href="/wiki/EU_Battlegroup" title="EU Battlegroup">EU Battlegroups</a> initiative, each of which is planned to be able to deploy quickly about 1500&#160;personnel.<sup id="cite_ref-Council_Capabilities_156-0" class="reference"><a href="#cite_note-Council_Capabilities-156">[137]</a></sup></p>
<p><a href="/wiki/EUFOR" class="mw-redirect" title="EUFOR">EU forces</a> have been deployed on peacekeeping missions from middle and northern Africa to the <a href="/wiki/Western_Balkans" class="mw-redirect" title="Western Balkans">western Balkans</a> and western Asia.<sup id="cite_ref-Council_Operations_157-0" class="reference"><a href="#cite_note-Council_Operations-157">[138]</a></sup> EU military operations are supported by a number of bodies, including the <a href="/wiki/European_Defence_Agency" title="European Defence Agency">European Defence Agency</a>, <a href="/wiki/European_Union_Satellite_Centre" title="European Union Satellite Centre">European Union Satellite Centre</a> and the <a href="/wiki/European_Union_Military_Staff" title="European Union Military Staff">European Union Military Staff</a>.<sup id="cite_ref-Council_Structures_158-0" class="reference"><a href="#cite_note-Council_Structures-158">[139]</a></sup> <a href="/wiki/Frontex" title="Frontex">Frontex</a> is an <a href="/wiki/Agencies_of_the_European_Union" title="Agencies of the European Union">agency of the EU</a> established to manage the cooperation between national <a href="/wiki/Border_guard" title="Border guard">border guards</a> securing its external <a href="/wiki/Border" title="Border">borders</a>. It aims to detect and stop illegal immigration, <a href="/wiki/Human_trafficking" title="Human trafficking">human trafficking</a> and terrorist infiltration. In 2015 the European Commission presented its proposal for a new European Border and Coast Guard Agency having a stronger role and mandate along with national authorities for border management. In an EU consisting of 28 members, substantial security and defence co-operation is increasingly relying on collaboration among all member states.<sup id="cite_ref-159" class="reference"><a href="#cite_note-159">[140]</a></sup></p>
<h3><span class="mw-headline" id="Humanitarian_aid">Humanitarian aid</span></h3>
<div role="note" class="hatnote">Further information: <a href="/wiki/Directorate-General_for_European_Civil_Protection_and_Humanitarian_Aid_Operations" title="Directorate-General for European Civil Protection and Humanitarian Aid Operations">Directorate-General for European Civil Protection and Humanitarian Aid Operations</a></div>
<div class="thumb tright">
<div class="thumbinner" style="width:222px;"><a href="/wiki/File:ECHO_plane.jpg" class="image"><img alt="" src="//upload.wikimedia.org/wikipedia/commons/thumb/1/19/ECHO_plane.jpg/220px-ECHO_plane.jpg" width="220" height="147" class="thumbimage" srcset="//upload.wikimedia.org/wikipedia/commons/thumb/1/19/ECHO_plane.jpg/330px-ECHO_plane.jpg 1.5x, //upload.wikimedia.org/wikipedia/commons/thumb/1/19/ECHO_plane.jpg/440px-ECHO_plane.jpg 2x" data-file-width="800" data-file-height="535" /></a>
<div class="thumbcaption">
<div class="magnify"><a href="/wiki/File:ECHO_plane.jpg" class="internal" title="Enlarge"></a></div>
Collectively, the EU is the largest contributor of <a href="/wiki/ECHO_(European_Commission)" class="mw-redirect" title="ECHO (European Commission)">foreign aid</a> in the world.<sup id="cite_ref-GHA_report_2014_160-0" class="reference"><a href="#cite_note-GHA_report_2014-160">[141]</a></sup><sup id="cite_ref-OECD_aid_report_2013_161-0" class="reference"><a href="#cite_note-OECD_aid_report_2013-161">[142]</a></sup></div>
</div>
</div>
<p>The <a href="/wiki/ECHO_(European_Commission)" class="mw-redirect" title="ECHO (European Commission)">European Commission's Humanitarian Aid and Civil Protection department</a>, or "ECHO", provides <a href="/wiki/Humanitarian_aid" title="Humanitarian aid">humanitarian aid</a> from the EU to <a href="/wiki/Developing_country" title="Developing country">developing countries</a>. In 2012, its budget amounted to €874 million, 51% of the budget went to Africa and 20% to Asia, Latin America, the Caribbean and Pacific, and 20% to the Middle East and Mediterranean.<sup id="cite_ref-162" class="reference"><a href="#cite_note-162">[143]</a></sup></p>
<div class="thumb tleft">
<div class="thumbinner" style="width:222px;"><a href="/wiki/File:IAHV,_Jordan_Program_At_Syrian_Refugee_Camp.jpg" class="image"><img alt="" src="//upload.wikimedia.org/wikipedia/commons/thumb/4/4a/IAHV%2C_Jordan_Program_At_Syrian_Refugee_Camp.jpg/220px-IAHV%2C_Jordan_Program_At_Syrian_Refugee_Camp.jpg" width="220" height="165" class="thumbimage" srcset="//upload.wikimedia.org/wikipedia/commons/thumb/4/4a/IAHV%2C_Jordan_Program_At_Syrian_Refugee_Camp.jpg/330px-IAHV%2C_Jordan_Program_At_Syrian_Refugee_Camp.jpg 1.5x, //upload.wikimedia.org/wikipedia/commons/thumb/4/4a/IAHV%2C_Jordan_Program_At_Syrian_Refugee_Camp.jpg/440px-IAHV%2C_Jordan_Program_At_Syrian_Refugee_Camp.jpg 2x" data-file-width="960" data-file-height="720" /></a>
<div class="thumbcaption">
<div class="magnify"><a href="/wiki/File:IAHV,_Jordan_Program_At_Syrian_Refugee_Camp.jpg" class="internal" title="Enlarge"></a></div>
The European Union co-funds <a href="/wiki/Psychosocial" title="Psychosocial">psychosocial</a> support by the IAHV, Jordan at the <a href="/wiki/Zaatari_refugee_camp" title="Zaatari refugee camp">Zaatari refugee camp</a> for the <a href="/wiki/Syrian_refugees" class="mw-redirect" title="Syrian refugees">Syrian refugees</a>.</div>
</div>
</div>
<p>Humanitarian aid is financed directly by the budget (70%) as part of the financial instruments for external action and also by the <a href="/wiki/European_Development_Fund" title="European Development Fund">European Development Fund</a> (30%).<sup id="cite_ref-ODI_163-0" class="reference"><a href="#cite_note-ODI-163">[144]</a></sup> The EU's external action financing is divided into 'geographic' instruments and 'thematic' instruments.<sup id="cite_ref-ODI_163-1" class="reference"><a href="#cite_note-ODI-163">[144]</a></sup> The 'geographic' instruments provide aid through the <a href="/wiki/Development_Cooperation_Instrument" title="Development Cooperation Instrument">Development Cooperation Instrument</a> (DCI, €16.9 billion, 2007–2013), which must spend 95% of its budget on <a href="/wiki/Department_for_International_Development" title="Department for International Development">overseas development assistance</a> (ODA), and from the <a href="/wiki/European_Neighbourhood_and_Partnership_Instrument" class="mw-redirect" title="European Neighbourhood and Partnership Instrument">European Neighbourhood and Partnership Instrument</a> (ENPI), which contains some relevant programmes.<sup id="cite_ref-ODI_163-2" class="reference"><a href="#cite_note-ODI-163">[144]</a></sup> The European Development Fund (EDF, €22.7 bn, 2008–2013) is made up of voluntary contributions by member states, but there is pressure to merge the EDF into the budget-financed instruments to encourage increased contributions to match the 0.7% target and allow the <a href="/wiki/European_Parliament" title="European Parliament">European Parliament</a> greater oversight.<sup id="cite_ref-ODI_163-3" class="reference"><a href="#cite_note-ODI-163">[144]</a></sup></p>
<p>However, five countries have reached the 0.7% target: Sweden, Luxembourg, the Netherlands, Denmark and the United Kingdom.<sup id="cite_ref-ODA_volumes:_Europe_and_the_world_164-0" class="reference"><a href="#cite_note-ODA_volumes:_Europe_and_the_world-164">[145]</a></sup><sup id="cite_ref-The_Guardian:_UK_meets_0.7.25_foreign_aid_target_165-0" class="reference"><a href="#cite_note-The_Guardian:_UK_meets_0.7.25_foreign_aid_target-165">[146]</a></sup></p>
<h2><span class="mw-headline" id="Economy">Economy</span></h2>
<div role="note" class="hatnote">Main articles: <a href="/wiki/Economy_of_the_European_Union" title="Economy of the European Union">Economy of the European Union</a> and <a href="/wiki/Regional_policy_of_the_European_Union" title="Regional policy of the European Union">Regional policy of the European Union</a></div>
<div class="thumbinner tright" style="width:302px; margin-left: 10px; margin-bottom: 10px;">
<div class="timeline-wrapper"><span style="display:inline-block; border:1px solid #ddd;"><map name="timeline_fa1ebd3b5720f6ccccd2c926bfbe040f"><img usemap="#timeline_fa1ebd3b5720f6ccccd2c926bfbe040f" src="//upload.wikimedia.org/wikipedia/en/timeline/fa1ebd3b5720f6ccccd2c926bfbe040f.png" /></map></span></div>
<div class="thumbcaption">The five largest economies in the world according to the <a href="/wiki/IMF" class="mw-redirect" title="IMF">IMF</a> by <a href="/wiki/List_of_countries_by_GDP_(nominal)" title="List of countries by GDP (nominal)">Nominal GDP</a> in 2015<sup id="cite_ref-166" class="reference"><a href="#cite_note-166">[147]</a></sup></div>
</div>
<p>The European Union has established a <a href="/wiki/Internal_market" class="mw-redirect" title="Internal market">single market</a> across the territory of all its members representing 510 million citizens. In 2014, the EU had a combined GDP of 18.640 trillion <a href="/wiki/International_dollar" class="mw-redirect" title="International dollar">international dollars</a>, a 20% share of <a href="/wiki/List_of_countries_by_GDP" class="mw-redirect" title="List of countries by GDP">global gross domestic product by purchasing power parity (PPP)</a>.<sup id="cite_ref-2011-IMG-GDP_167-0" class="reference"><a href="#cite_note-2011-IMG-GDP-167">[148]</a></sup> As a political entity the European Union is represented in the <a href="/wiki/World_Trade_Organization" title="World Trade Organization">World Trade Organization</a> (WTO). EU member states own the estimated largest <a href="/wiki/Net_wealth" class="mw-redirect" title="Net wealth">net wealth</a> in the world, equal to 30% of the $223 trillion global wealth.<sup class="noprint Inline-Template Template-Fact" style="white-space:nowrap;">[<i><a href="/wiki/Wikipedia:Citation_needed" title="Wikipedia:Citation needed"><span title="This claim needs references to reliable sources. (January 2017)">citation needed</span></a></i>]</sup></p>
<p>19 member states have joined a monetary union known as the <a href="/wiki/Eurozone" title="Eurozone">eurozone</a>, which uses the <a href="/wiki/Euro" title="Euro">Euro</a> as a single currency. The currency union represents 338 million EU citizens.<sup id="cite_ref-Europa_Single_Market_168-0" class="reference"><a href="#cite_note-Europa_Single_Market-168">[149]</a></sup> The euro is the second largest <a href="/wiki/Reserve_currency" title="Reserve currency">reserve currency</a> as well as the second most traded currency in the world after the <a href="/wiki/United_States_dollar" title="United States dollar">United States dollar</a>.<sup id="cite_ref-169" class="reference"><a href="#cite_note-169">[150]</a></sup><sup id="cite_ref-170" class="reference"><a href="#cite_note-170">[151]</a></sup><sup id="cite_ref-171" class="reference"><a href="#cite_note-171">[152]</a></sup></p>
<p>Of the top <a href="/wiki/Fortune_Global_500" title="Fortune Global 500">500 largest corporations in the world measured by revenue</a> in 2010, 161 have their headquarters in the EU.<sup id="cite_ref-172" class="reference"><a href="#cite_note-172">[153]</a></sup> In 2016, unemployment in the EU stood at 8.9%<sup id="cite_ref-UNEMP_173-0" class="reference"><a href="#cite_note-UNEMP-173">[154]</a></sup> while inflation was at 2.2%, and the current account balance at −0.9% of GDP. The average annual net wage in the European Union was around $20,000 in 2015, which was about half of that in the <a href="/wiki/United_States" title="United States">United States</a>.<sup id="cite_ref-174" class="reference"><a href="#cite_note-174">[155]</a></sup></p>
<p>There is a significant variance for GDP (PPP) per capita within individual EU states. The difference between the richest and poorest regions (276 NUTS-2 regions of the <a href="/wiki/Nomenclature_of_Territorial_Units_for_Statistics" title="Nomenclature of Territorial Units for Statistics">Nomenclature of Territorial Units for Statistics</a>) ranged, in 2014, from 30% of the EU28 average to 539%, or from €8,200 to €148,000 (about US$9,000 to US$162,000).<sup id="cite_ref-eurostat14_175-0" class="reference"><a href="#cite_note-eurostat14-175">[156]</a></sup></p>
<p><a href="/wiki/Structural_Funds_and_Cohesion_Funds" class="mw-redirect" title="Structural Funds and Cohesion Funds">Structural Funds and Cohesion Funds</a> are supporting the development of underdeveloped regions of the EU. Such regions are primarily located in the states of <a href="/wiki/Central_Europe" title="Central Europe">central</a> and southern Europe.<sup id="cite_ref-176" class="reference"><a href="#cite_note-176">[157]</a></sup><sup id="cite_ref-Business2000_Funds_177-0" class="reference"><a href="#cite_note-Business2000_Funds-177">[158]</a></sup> Several funds provide emergency aid, support for candidate members to transform their country to conform to the EU's standard (<a href="/wiki/Phare" title="Phare">Phare</a>, <a href="/wiki/Instrument_for_Structural_Policies_for_Pre-Accession" title="Instrument for Structural Policies for Pre-Accession">ISPA</a>, and <a href="/wiki/Special_Accession_Programme_for_Agriculture_and_Rural_Development" title="Special Accession Programme for Agriculture and Rural Development">SAPARD</a>), and support to the <a href="/wiki/Commonwealth_of_Independent_States" title="Commonwealth of Independent States">Commonwealth of Independent States</a> (<a href="/wiki/Technical_Aid_to_the_Commonwealth_of_Independent_States" title="Technical Aid to the Commonwealth of Independent States">TACIS</a>). TACIS has now become part of the worldwide <a href="/wiki/EuropeAid_Co-operation_Office" class="mw-redirect" title="EuropeAid Co-operation Office">EuropeAid</a> programme. EU <a href="/wiki/Framework_Programmes_for_Research_and_Technological_Development" title="Framework Programmes for Research and Technological Development">research and technological framework programmes</a> sponsor research conducted by consortia from all EU members to work towards a single <a href="/wiki/European_Research_Area" title="European Research Area">European Research Area</a>.<sup id="cite_ref-Euractiv_FP7_178-0" class="reference"><a href="#cite_note-Euractiv_FP7-178">[159]</a></sup></p>
<h3><span class="mw-headline" id="Internal_market">Internal market</span></h3>
<div role="note" class="hatnote">Main article: <a href="/wiki/European_Single_Market" title="European Single Market">European Single Market</a></div>
<div class="thumb tright">
<div class="thumbinner" style="width:172px;"><a href="/wiki/File:Eirepas.JPG" class="image"><img alt="" src="//upload.wikimedia.org/wikipedia/commons/thumb/d/d7/Eirepas.JPG/170px-Eirepas.JPG" width="170" height="239" class="thumbimage" srcset="//upload.wikimedia.org/wikipedia/commons/thumb/d/d7/Eirepas.JPG/255px-Eirepas.JPG 1.5x, //upload.wikimedia.org/wikipedia/commons/thumb/d/d7/Eirepas.JPG/340px-Eirepas.JPG 2x" data-file-width="600" data-file-height="842" /></a>
<div class="thumbcaption">
<div class="magnify"><a href="/wiki/File:Eirepas.JPG" class="internal" title="Enlarge"></a></div>
A <a href="/wiki/Passports_of_the_European_Union" title="Passports of the European Union">standardised passport</a> design, displaying the name of the member state, the national <a href="/wiki/Coat_of_arms" title="Coat of arms">arms</a> and the words "European Union" given in their official language(s). (Irish model)</div>
</div>
</div>
<p>Two of the original core objectives of the European Economic Community were the development of a common market, subsequently becoming a <a href="/wiki/Single_market" title="Single market">single market</a>, and a <a href="/wiki/European_Union_Customs_Union" title="European Union Customs Union">customs union</a> between its member states. The single market involves <a href="/wiki/Internal_market" class="mw-redirect" title="Internal market">the free circulation of goods, capital, people, and services within the EU</a>,<sup id="cite_ref-Europa_Single_Market_168-1" class="reference"><a href="#cite_note-Europa_Single_Market-168">[149]</a></sup> and the customs union involves the application of a <a href="/wiki/Common_external_tariff" title="Common external tariff">common external tariff</a> on all goods entering the market. Once goods have been admitted into the market they cannot be subjected to customs duties, discriminatory taxes or <a href="/wiki/Import_quota" title="Import quota">import quotas</a>, as they travel internally. The non-EU member states of <a href="/wiki/Iceland" title="Iceland">Iceland</a>, <a href="/wiki/Norway" title="Norway">Norway</a>, <a href="/wiki/Liechtenstein" title="Liechtenstein">Liechtenstein</a> and <a href="/wiki/Switzerland" title="Switzerland">Switzerland</a> participate in the single market but not in the customs union.<sup id="cite_ref-EEA_66-1" class="reference"><a href="#cite_note-EEA-66">[59]</a></sup> Half the trade in the EU is covered by legislation harmonised by the EU.<sup id="cite_ref-Europa_Single_Market_G_179-0" class="reference"><a href="#cite_note-Europa_Single_Market_G-179">[160]</a></sup></p>
<p>Free movement of capital is intended to permit movement of investments such as property purchases and buying of shares between countries.<sup id="cite_ref-Europa_Single_Market_C_180-0" class="reference"><a href="#cite_note-Europa_Single_Market_C-180">[161]</a></sup> Until the drive towards <a href="/wiki/Economic_and_monetary_union" title="Economic and monetary union">economic and monetary union</a> the development of the capital provisions had been slow. Post-Maastricht there has been a rapidly developing corpus of ECJ judgements regarding this initially neglected freedom. The free movement of capital is unique insofar as it is granted equally to non-member states.</p>
<p>The free movement of persons means that <a href="/wiki/Citizenship_of_the_European_Union" title="Citizenship of the European Union">EU citizens</a> can move freely between member states to live, work, study or retire in another country. This required the lowering of administrative formalities and recognition of professional qualifications of other states.<sup id="cite_ref-Europa_Single_Market_P_181-0" class="reference"><a href="#cite_note-Europa_Single_Market_P-181">[162]</a></sup></p>
<p>The free movement of services and of establishment allows self-employed persons to move between member states to provide services on a temporary or permanent basis. While services account for 60–70% of GDP, legislation in the area is not as developed as in other areas. This lacuna has been addressed by the recently passed <a href="/wiki/Directive_on_services_in_the_internal_market" class="mw-redirect" title="Directive on services in the internal market">Directive on services in the internal market</a> which aims to liberalise the cross border provision of services.<sup id="cite_ref-Europa_Single_Market_S_182-0" class="reference"><a href="#cite_note-Europa_Single_Market_S-182">[163]</a></sup> According to the Treaty the provision of services is a residual freedom that only applies if no other freedom is being exercised.</p>
<h3><span class="mw-headline" id="Monetary_union">Monetary union</span></h3>
<div role="note" class="hatnote">Main articles: <a href="/wiki/Eurozone" title="Eurozone">Eurozone</a> and <a href="/wiki/Economic_and_Monetary_Union_of_the_European_Union" title="Economic and Monetary Union of the European Union">Economic and Monetary Union of the European Union</a></div>
<div class="thumb tright">
<div class="thumbinner" style="width:172px;"><a href="/wiki/File:European_Central_Bank_-_building_under_construction_-_Frankfurt_-_Germany_-_13.jpg" class="image"><img alt="" src="//upload.wikimedia.org/wikipedia/commons/thumb/5/51/European_Central_Bank_-_building_under_construction_-_Frankfurt_-_Germany_-_13.jpg/170px-European_Central_Bank_-_building_under_construction_-_Frankfurt_-_Germany_-_13.jpg" width="170" height="282" class="thumbimage" srcset="//upload.wikimedia.org/wikipedia/commons/thumb/5/51/European_Central_Bank_-_building_under_construction_-_Frankfurt_-_Germany_-_13.jpg/255px-European_Central_Bank_-_building_under_construction_-_Frankfurt_-_Germany_-_13.jpg 1.5x, //upload.wikimedia.org/wikipedia/commons/thumb/5/51/European_Central_Bank_-_building_under_construction_-_Frankfurt_-_Germany_-_13.jpg/340px-European_Central_Bank_-_building_under_construction_-_Frankfurt_-_Germany_-_13.jpg 2x" data-file-width="3622" data-file-height="6000" /></a>
<div class="thumbcaption">
<div class="magnify"><a href="/wiki/File:European_Central_Bank_-_building_under_construction_-_Frankfurt_-_Germany_-_13.jpg" class="internal" title="Enlarge"></a></div>
The <a href="/wiki/Seat_of_the_European_Central_Bank" title="Seat of the European Central Bank">seat</a> of the <a href="/wiki/European_Central_Bank" title="European Central Bank">European Central Bank</a> in <a href="/wiki/Frankfurt" title="Frankfurt">Frankfurt</a>. 19 of the 28 EU member states have adopted the <a href="/wiki/Euro" title="Euro">euro</a> as their <a href="/wiki/Legal_tender" title="Legal tender">legal tender</a>.</div>
</div>
</div>
<p>The creation of a <a href="/wiki/European_Currency_Unit" title="European Currency Unit">European single currency</a> became an official objective of the European Economic Community in 1969. In 1992, having negotiated the structure and procedures of a currency union, the member states signed the <a href="/wiki/Maastricht_Treaty" title="Maastricht Treaty">Maastricht Treaty</a> and were legally bound to fulfil the agreed-on rules including the <a href="/wiki/Euro_convergence_criteria" title="Euro convergence criteria">convergence criteria</a> if they wanted to join the <a href="/wiki/Currency_union" title="Currency union">monetary union</a>. The states wanting to participate had first to join the <a href="/wiki/European_Exchange_Rate_Mechanism" title="European Exchange Rate Mechanism">European Exchange Rate Mechanism</a>.</p>
<p>In 1999 the currency union started, first as an accounting currency with <a href="/wiki/History_of_the_euro" title="History of the euro">eleven member states</a> joining. In 2002, the currency was fully put into place, when <a href="/wiki/Euro_banknotes" title="Euro banknotes">euro notes</a> and coins were issued and national currencies began to phase out in the eurozone, which by then consisted of 12 member states. The eurozone (constituted by the EU member states which have adopted the euro) has since grown to 19 countries.<sup id="cite_ref-183" class="reference"><a href="#cite_note-183">[164]</a></sup><sup id="cite_ref-184" class="reference"><a href="#cite_note-184">[t]</a></sup></p>
<div class="thumb tleft">
<div class="thumbinner" style="width:222px;"><a href="/wiki/File:BlueEurozone.svg" class="image"><img alt="" src="//upload.wikimedia.org/wikipedia/commons/thumb/6/65/BlueEurozone.svg/220px-BlueEurozone.svg.png" width="220" height="235" class="thumbimage" srcset="//upload.wikimedia.org/wikipedia/commons/thumb/6/65/BlueEurozone.svg/330px-BlueEurozone.svg.png 1.5x, //upload.wikimedia.org/wikipedia/commons/thumb/6/65/BlueEurozone.svg/440px-BlueEurozone.svg.png 2x" data-file-width="645" data-file-height="690" /></a>
<div class="thumbcaption">
<div class="magnify"><a href="/wiki/File:BlueEurozone.svg" class="internal" title="Enlarge"></a></div>
The <a href="/wiki/Eurozone" title="Eurozone">Eurozone</a> (dark blue) represents 340 million people. The euro is the second-largest reserve currency in the world.</div>
</div>
</div>
<p>The euro, and the monetary policies of those who have adopted it in agreement with the EU, are under the control of the <a href="/wiki/European_Central_Bank" title="European Central Bank">European Central Bank</a> (ECB).<sup id="cite_ref-185" class="reference"><a href="#cite_note-185">[165]</a></sup> The ECB is the central bank for the eurozone, and thus controls <a href="/wiki/Monetary_policy" title="Monetary policy">monetary policy</a> in that area with an agenda to maintain <a href="/wiki/Price_stability" class="mw-redirect" title="Price stability">price stability</a>. It is at the centre of the <a href="/wiki/European_System_of_Central_Banks" title="European System of Central Banks">European System of Central Banks</a>, which comprehends all EU national central banks and is controlled by its General Council, consisting of the <a href="/wiki/President_of_the_European_Central_Bank" title="President of the European Central Bank">President of the ECB</a>, who is appointed by the European Council, the Vice-President of the ECB, and the governors of the national central banks of all 28 EU member states.<sup id="cite_ref-ECB_org_186-0" class="reference"><a href="#cite_note-ECB_org-186">[166]</a></sup></p>
<p>The <a href="/wiki/European_System_of_Financial_Supervision" title="European System of Financial Supervision">European System of Financial Supervision</a> is an institutional architecture of the EU's framework of financial supervision composed by three authorities: the <a href="/wiki/European_Banking_Authority" title="European Banking Authority">European Banking Authority</a>, the <a href="/wiki/European_Insurance_and_Occupational_Pensions_Authority" title="European Insurance and Occupational Pensions Authority">European Insurance and Occupational Pensions Authority</a> and the <a href="/wiki/European_Securities_and_Markets_Authority" title="European Securities and Markets Authority">European Securities and Markets Authority</a>. To complement this framework, there is also a <a href="/wiki/European_Systemic_Risk_Board" title="European Systemic Risk Board">European Systemic Risk Board</a> under the responsibility of the <a href="/wiki/European_Central_Bank" title="European Central Bank">ECB</a>. The aim of this financial control system is to ensure the economic stability of the EU.<sup id="cite_ref-187" class="reference"><a href="#cite_note-187">[167]</a></sup></p>
<p>To prevent the joining states from getting into financial trouble or crisis after entering the monetary union, they were obliged in the Maastricht treaty to fulfil important financial obligations and procedures, especially to show budgetary discipline and a high degree of sustainable economic convergence, as well as to avoid excessive government deficits and limit the government debt to a sustainable level.</p>
<h3><span class="mw-headline" id="Energy">Energy</span></h3>
<div role="note" class="hatnote">Main article: <a href="/wiki/Energy_policy_of_the_European_Union" title="Energy policy of the European Union">Energy policy of the European Union</a></div>
<div class="barbox tright" style="border:1px solid silver; font-size:88%; padding:0.4em; width:300px; background: white;">
<table style="border-collapse:collapse; width:100%;">

<th colspan="5">Consumed energy (2012)<sup id="cite_ref-Discrimination_in_the_EU_in_2012_188-0" class="reference"><a href="#cite_note-Discrimination_in_the_EU_in_2012-188">[168]</a></sup></th>

</div>
<p>In 2006, the <a href="/wiki/Treaty_of_Accession_2005" title="Treaty of Accession 2005">EU-27</a> had a gross inland energy consumption of 1,825&#160;million tonnes of oil equivalent (toe).<sup id="cite_ref-EurostatEnergy_190-0" class="reference"><a href="#cite_note-EurostatEnergy-190">[169]</a></sup> Around 46% of the energy consumed was produced within the member states while 54% was imported.<sup id="cite_ref-EurostatEnergy_190-1" class="reference"><a href="#cite_note-EurostatEnergy-190">[169]</a></sup> In these statistics, nuclear energy is treated as <a href="/wiki/Primary_energy" title="Primary energy">primary energy</a> produced in the EU, regardless of the source of the uranium, of which less than 3% is produced in the EU.<sup id="cite_ref-Euratom2007_191-0" class="reference"><a href="#cite_note-Euratom2007-191">[170]</a></sup></p>
<p>The EU has had legislative power in the area of energy policy for most of its existence; this has its roots in the original <a href="/wiki/European_Coal_and_Steel_Community" title="European Coal and Steel Community">European Coal and Steel Community</a>. The introduction of a mandatory and comprehensive European energy policy was approved at the meeting of the European Council in October 2005, and the first draft policy was published in January 2007.<sup id="cite_ref-Energy_Q.26A_192-0" class="reference"><a href="#cite_note-Energy_Q.26A-192">[171]</a></sup></p>
<p>The EU has five key points in its energy policy: increase competition in the <a href="/wiki/Internal_market" class="mw-redirect" title="Internal market">internal market</a>, encourage investment and boost interconnections between electricity grids; diversify energy resources with better systems to respond to a crisis; establish a new treaty framework for energy co-operation with Russia while improving relations with energy-rich states in Central Asia<sup id="cite_ref-oies_193-0" class="reference"><a href="#cite_note-oies-193">[172]</a></sup> and North Africa; use existing energy supplies more efficiently while increasing <a href="/wiki/Renewable_energy_commercialisation" class="mw-redirect" title="Renewable energy commercialisation">renewable energy commercialisation</a>; and finally increase funding for new energy technologies.<sup id="cite_ref-Energy_Q.26A_192-1" class="reference"><a href="#cite_note-Energy_Q.26A-192">[171]</a></sup></p>
<p>In 2007, EU countries as a whole imported 82% of their oil, 57% of their natural gas<sup id="cite_ref-low_carb_prop_194-0" class="reference"><a href="#cite_note-low_carb_prop-194">[173]</a></sup> and 97.48% of their uranium<sup id="cite_ref-Euratom2007_191-1" class="reference"><a href="#cite_note-Euratom2007-191">[170]</a></sup> demands. There is a strong <a href="/wiki/Russia_in_the_European_energy_sector" title="Russia in the European energy sector">dependence on Russian energy</a> that the EU has been attempting to reduce.<sup id="cite_ref-Energy_Russia_195-0" class="reference"><a href="#cite_note-Energy_Russia-195">[174]</a></sup></p>
<h3><span class="mw-headline" id="Infrastructure">Infrastructure</span></h3>
<div role="note" class="hatnote">Further information: <a href="/wiki/European_Commissioner_for_Transport" title="European Commissioner for Transport">European Commissioner for Transport</a>, <a href="/wiki/European_Commissioner_for_Industry_and_Entrepreneurship" title="European Commissioner for Industry and Entrepreneurship">European Commissioner for Industry and Entrepreneurship</a>, and <a href="/wiki/European_Investment_Bank" title="European Investment Bank">European Investment Bank</a></div>
<div class="thumb tleft">
<div class="thumbinner" style="width:222px;"><a href="/wiki/File:%C3%96resundsbron_i_solnedg%C3%A5ng_2.jpg" class="image"><img alt="" src="//upload.wikimedia.org/wikipedia/commons/thumb/0/01/%C3%96resundsbron_i_solnedg%C3%A5ng_2.jpg/220px-%C3%96resundsbron_i_solnedg%C3%A5ng_2.jpg" width="220" height="143" class="thumbimage" srcset="//upload.wikimedia.org/wikipedia/commons/thumb/0/01/%C3%96resundsbron_i_solnedg%C3%A5ng_2.jpg/330px-%C3%96resundsbron_i_solnedg%C3%A5ng_2.jpg 1.5x, //upload.wikimedia.org/wikipedia/commons/thumb/0/01/%C3%96resundsbron_i_solnedg%C3%A5ng_2.jpg/440px-%C3%96resundsbron_i_solnedg%C3%A5ng_2.jpg 2x" data-file-width="1404" data-file-height="912" /></a>
<div class="thumbcaption">
<div class="magnify"><a href="/wiki/File:%C3%96resundsbron_i_solnedg%C3%A5ng_2.jpg" class="internal" title="Enlarge"></a></div>
The <a href="/wiki/%C3%98resund_Bridge" title="Øresund Bridge">Öresund Bridge</a> between Denmark and Sweden is part of the <a href="/wiki/Trans-European_Networks" title="Trans-European Networks">Trans-European Networks</a>.</div>
</div>
</div>
<p>The EU is working to improve cross-border infrastructure within the EU, for example through the <a href="/wiki/Trans-European_Networks" title="Trans-European Networks">Trans-European Networks</a> (TEN). Projects under TEN include the <a href="/wiki/Channel_Tunnel" title="Channel Tunnel">Channel Tunnel</a>, <a href="/wiki/LGV_Est" title="LGV Est">LGV Est</a>, the <a href="/wiki/Fr%C3%A9jus_Rail_Tunnel" title="Fréjus Rail Tunnel">Fréjus Rail Tunnel</a>, the <a href="/wiki/%C3%98resund_Bridge" title="Øresund Bridge">Öresund Bridge</a>, the <a href="/wiki/Brenner_Base_Tunnel" title="Brenner Base Tunnel">Brenner Base Tunnel</a> and the <a href="/wiki/Strait_of_Messina_Bridge" title="Strait of Messina Bridge">Strait of Messina Bridge</a>. In 2010 the estimated network covers: 75,200 kilometres (46,700&#160;mi) of roads; 78,000 kilometres (48,000&#160;mi) of railways; 330 airports; 270 maritime harbours; and 210 internal harbours.<sup id="cite_ref-196" class="reference"><a href="#cite_note-196">[175]</a></sup><sup id="cite_ref-197" class="reference"><a href="#cite_note-197">[176]</a></sup></p>
<p><a href="/wiki/Rail_transport_in_Europe" title="Rail transport in Europe">Rail transport in Europe</a> is being synchronised with the <a href="/wiki/European_Rail_Traffic_Management_System" title="European Rail Traffic Management System">European Rail Traffic Management System</a> (ERTMS), an initiative to greatly enhance safety, increase efficiency of trains and enhance cross-border interoperability of rail transport in Europe by replacing signalling equipment with digitised mostly wireless versions and by creating a single Europe-wide standard for train control and command systems.</p>
<p>The developing European transport policies will increase the pressure on the environment in many regions by the increased transport network. In the pre-2004 EU members, the major problem in transport deals with congestion and pollution. After the recent enlargement, the new states that joined since 2004 added the problem of solving accessibility to the transport agenda.<sup id="cite_ref-198" class="reference"><a href="#cite_note-198">[177]</a></sup> The <a href="/wiki/Highways_in_Poland" title="Highways in Poland">Polish road network</a> was upgraded such as the <a href="/wiki/A4_autostrada_(Poland)" title="A4 autostrada (Poland)">A4 autostrada</a>.<sup id="cite_ref-199" class="reference"><a href="#cite_note-199">[178]</a></sup><sup id="cite_ref-200" class="reference"><a href="#cite_note-200">[179]</a></sup></p>
<p>The <a href="/wiki/Galileo_(satellite_navigation)" title="Galileo (satellite navigation)">Galileo positioning system</a> is another EU infrastructure project. Galileo is a proposed <a href="/wiki/Satellite_navigation" title="Satellite navigation">Satellite navigation</a> system, to be built by the EU and launched by the <a href="/wiki/European_Space_Agency" title="European Space Agency">European Space Agency</a> (ESA). The Galileo project was launched partly to reduce the EU's dependency on the US-operated <a href="/wiki/Global_Positioning_System" title="Global Positioning System">Global Positioning System</a>, but also to give more complete global coverage and allow for greater accuracy, given the aged nature of the GPS system.<sup id="cite_ref-Barrot_201-0" class="reference"><a href="#cite_note-Barrot-201">[180]</a></sup></p>
<div style="clear:left;"></div>
<h3><span class="mw-headline" id="Agriculture">Agriculture</span></h3>
<div role="note" class="hatnote">Main article: <a href="/wiki/Common_Agricultural_Policy" title="Common Agricultural Policy">Common Agricultural Policy</a></div>
<div class="thumb tright">
<div class="thumbinner" style="width:222px;"><a href="/wiki/File:RO_BZ_Pietroasele_vineyard.jpg" class="image"><img alt="" src="//upload.wikimedia.org/wikipedia/commons/thumb/4/4d/RO_BZ_Pietroasele_vineyard.jpg/220px-RO_BZ_Pietroasele_vineyard.jpg" width="220" height="147" class="thumbimage" srcset="//upload.wikimedia.org/wikipedia/commons/thumb/4/4d/RO_BZ_Pietroasele_vineyard.jpg/330px-RO_BZ_Pietroasele_vineyard.jpg 1.5x, //upload.wikimedia.org/wikipedia/commons/thumb/4/4d/RO_BZ_Pietroasele_vineyard.jpg/440px-RO_BZ_Pietroasele_vineyard.jpg 2x" data-file-width="3872" data-file-height="2592" /></a>
<div class="thumbcaption">
<div class="magnify"><a href="/wiki/File:RO_BZ_Pietroasele_vineyard.jpg" class="internal" title="Enlarge"></a></div>
Vineyards in <a href="/wiki/Romania" title="Romania">Romania</a>; EU farms are supported by the <a href="/wiki/Common_Agricultural_Policy" title="Common Agricultural Policy">Common Agricultural Policy</a>, the largest <a href="/wiki/Budget_of_the_European_Union" title="Budget of the European Union">budgetary</a> expenditure.</div>
</div>
</div>
<p>The <a href="/wiki/Common_Agricultural_Policy" title="Common Agricultural Policy">Common Agricultural Policy</a> (CAP) is one of the long lasting policies of the European Community.<sup id="cite_ref-stead_202-0" class="reference"><a href="#cite_note-stead-202">[181]</a></sup> The policy has the objectives of increasing agricultural production, providing certainty in food supplies, ensuring a high quality of life for farmers, stabilising markets, and ensuring reasonable prices for consumers.<sup id="cite_ref-203" class="reference"><a href="#cite_note-203">[v]</a></sup> It was, until recently, operated by a system of subsidies and market intervention. Until the 1990s, the policy accounted for over 60% of the then <a href="/wiki/European_Economic_Community" title="European Economic Community">European Community</a>'s annual budget, and as of 2013<sup class="plainlinks noprint asof-tag update" style="display:none;"><a class="external text" href="//en.wikipedia.org/w/index.php?title=European_Union&amp;action=edit">[update]</a></sup> accounts for around 34%.<sup id="cite_ref-EU_CAP_204-0" class="reference"><a href="#cite_note-EU_CAP-204">[182]</a></sup><sup class="noprint Inline-Template" style="white-space:nowrap;">[<i><a href="/wiki/Wikipedia:Verifiability" title="Wikipedia:Verifiability"><span title="The material near this tag failed verification of its source citation(s). (January 2017)">not in citation given</span></a></i>]</sup></p>
<p>The policy's <a href="/wiki/Incomes_policy" title="Incomes policy">price controls</a> and market interventions led to considerable overproduction. These were <a href="/wiki/Buffer_stock_scheme" title="Buffer stock scheme">intervention stores</a> of products bought up by the Community to maintain minimum price levels. To dispose of surplus stores, they were often sold on the world market at prices considerably below Community guaranteed prices, or farmers were offered subsidies (amounting to the difference between the Community and world prices) to export their products outside the Community. This system has been criticised for under-cutting farmers outside Europe, especially those in the <a href="/wiki/Third_World" title="Third World">developing world</a>.<sup id="cite_ref-Guardian_CAP_205-0" class="reference"><a href="#cite_note-Guardian_CAP-205">[183]</a></sup> Supporters of CAP argue that the economic support which it gives to farmers provides them with a reasonable standard of living.<sup id="cite_ref-Guardian_CAP_205-1" class="reference"><a href="#cite_note-Guardian_CAP-205">[183]</a></sup></p>
<p>Since the beginning of the 1990s, the CAP has been subject to a series of reforms. Initially, these reforms included the introduction of <a href="/wiki/Set-aside" title="Set-aside">set-aside</a> in 1988, where a proportion of farm land was deliberately withdrawn from production, milk quotas and, more recently, the 'de-coupling' (or disassociation) of the money farmers receive from the EU and the amount they produce (by the Fischler reforms in 2004). Agriculture expenditure will move away from subsidy payments linked to specific produce, toward direct payments based on farm size. This is intended to allow the market to dictate production levels.<sup id="cite_ref-stead_202-1" class="reference"><a href="#cite_note-stead-202">[181]</a></sup> One of these reforms entailed the modification of the EU's sugar regime, which previously divided the sugar market between member states and certain African-Caribbean nations with a privileged relationship with the EU.<sup id="cite_ref-EC_CAP_206-0" class="reference"><a href="#cite_note-EC_CAP-206">[184]</a></sup></p>
<h3><span class="mw-headline" id="Competition">Competition</span></h3>
<div role="note" class="hatnote">Further information: <a href="/wiki/European_Union_competition_law" title="European Union competition law">European Union competition law</a> and <a href="/wiki/European_Commissioner_for_Competition" title="European Commissioner for Competition">European Commissioner for Competition</a></div>
<p>The EU operates a <a href="/wiki/European_Union_competition_law" title="European Union competition law">competition policy</a> intended to ensure undistorted competition within the single market.<sup id="cite_ref-207" class="reference"><a href="#cite_note-207">[w]</a></sup> The Commission as the competition regulator for the single market is responsible for antitrust issues, approving <a href="/wiki/Mergers_and_acquisitions" title="Mergers and acquisitions">mergers</a>, breaking up cartels, working for <a href="/wiki/Economic_liberalism" title="Economic liberalism">economic liberalisation</a> and preventing <a href="/wiki/Subsidy" title="Subsidy">state aid</a>.<sup id="cite_ref-208" class="reference"><a href="#cite_note-208">[185]</a></sup><sup class="noprint Inline-Template" style="white-space:nowrap;">[<i><a href="/wiki/Wikipedia:Verifiability" title="Wikipedia:Verifiability"><span title="The material near this tag failed verification of its source citation(s). (January 2017)">not in citation given</span></a></i>]</sup></p>
<p>The <a href="/wiki/European_Commissioner_for_Competition" title="European Commissioner for Competition">Competition Commissioner</a>, currently <a href="/wiki/Margrethe_Vestager" title="Margrethe Vestager">Margrethe Vestager</a>, is one of the most powerful positions in the Commission, notable for the ability to affect the commercial interests of trans-national corporations.<sup id="cite_ref-209" class="reference"><a href="#cite_note-209">[186]</a></sup><sup class="noprint Inline-Template" style="white-space:nowrap;">[<i><a href="/wiki/Wikipedia:Verifiability" title="Wikipedia:Verifiability"><span title="The material near this tag failed verification of its source citation(s). (January 2017)">not in citation given</span></a></i>]</sup> For example, in 2001 the Commission for the first time prevented a merger between two companies based in the United States (<a href="/wiki/General_Electric" title="General Electric">GE</a> and <a href="/wiki/Honeywell" title="Honeywell">Honeywell</a>) which had already been approved by their national authority.<sup id="cite_ref-210" class="reference"><a href="#cite_note-210">[187]</a></sup> Another high-profile case <a href="/wiki/European_Union_Microsoft_competition_case" class="mw-redirect" title="European Union Microsoft competition case">against Microsoft</a>, resulted in the Commission fining <a href="/wiki/Microsoft" title="Microsoft">Microsoft</a> over €777&#160;million following nine years of legal action.<sup id="cite_ref-211" class="reference"><a href="#cite_note-211">[188]</a></sup></p>
<h2><span class="mw-headline" id="Demographics">Demographics</span></h2>
<div role="note" class="hatnote">Main article: <a href="/wiki/Demographics_of_the_European_Union" title="Demographics of the European Union">Demographics of the European Union</a></div>
<p>As of 1 January 2016, the population of the European Union is about 510.1&#160;million people.<sup id="cite_ref-population_9-2" class="reference"><a href="#cite_note-population-9">[6]</a></sup> In 2013, 5,075,000 live births were registered and 4,999,200 deaths. The net migration to the EU was +653,100. In 2010, 47.3 million people who lived in the EU were born outside their resident country. This corresponds to 9.4% of the total EU population. Of these, 31.4 million (6.3%) were born outside the EU and 16.0 million (3.2%) were born in another EU member state. The largest absolute numbers of people born outside the EU were in Germany (6.4 million), France (5.1 million), the United Kingdom (4.7 million), Spain (4.1 million), Italy (3.2 million), and the Netherlands (1.4 million).<sup id="cite_ref-212" class="reference"><a href="#cite_note-212">[189]</a></sup></p>
<h3><span class="mw-headline" id="Urbanisation">Urbanisation</span></h3>
<p>The EU contains about 40 urban areas with populations of over one million. In addition to large <a href="/wiki/Urban_agglomeration" title="Urban agglomeration">agglomerations</a>, the EU also includes several densely populated polycentric urbanised regions that have no single core but have emerged from the connection of several cities and now encompass a large <a href="/wiki/Metropolis" title="Metropolis">metropolis</a>. The largest of these polycentric metropolis include <a href="/wiki/Rhine-Ruhr" title="Rhine-Ruhr">Rhine-Ruhr</a> with approximately 11.5&#160;million inhabitants (<a href="/wiki/Cologne" title="Cologne">Cologne</a>, <a href="/wiki/Dortmund" title="Dortmund">Dortmund</a>, <a href="/wiki/D%C3%BCsseldorf" title="Düsseldorf">Düsseldorf</a> et al.), <a href="/wiki/Randstad" title="Randstad">Randstad</a> with approx. 7&#160;million (<a href="/wiki/Amsterdam" title="Amsterdam">Amsterdam</a>, <a href="/wiki/Rotterdam" title="Rotterdam">Rotterdam</a>, <a href="/wiki/The_Hague" title="The Hague">The Hague</a>, <a href="/wiki/Utrecht" title="Utrecht">Utrecht</a> et al.), <a href="/wiki/Frankfurt_Rhine-Main" title="Frankfurt Rhine-Main">Frankfurt Rhine-Main</a> with approx. 5.8&#160;million (<a href="/wiki/Frankfurt" title="Frankfurt">Frankfurt</a>, <a href="/wiki/Wiesbaden" title="Wiesbaden">Wiesbaden</a> et al.), the <a href="/wiki/Flemish_Diamond" title="Flemish Diamond">Flemish Diamond</a> with approx. 5.5&#160;million (<a href="/wiki/Antwerp" title="Antwerp">Antwerp</a>, <a href="/wiki/Brussels" title="Brussels">Brussels</a>, <a href="/wiki/Leuven" title="Leuven">Leuven</a>, <a href="/wiki/Ghent" title="Ghent">Ghent</a> et al.), <a href="/wiki/Upper_Silesian_metropolitan_area" title="Upper Silesian metropolitan area">Upper Silesia</a> with approx. 5.3&#160;million (<a href="/wiki/Katowice" title="Katowice">Katowice</a>, <a href="/wiki/Ostrava" title="Ostrava">Ostrava</a>) and <a href="/wiki/%C3%98resund_Region" title="Øresund Region">Øresund</a> with approx. 3.7&#160;million (<a href="/wiki/Copenhagen" title="Copenhagen">Copenhagen</a>, <a href="/wiki/Malm%C3%B6" title="Malmö">Malmö</a>).<sup id="cite_ref-Eurostat_Urban_zones_213-0" class="reference"><a href="#cite_note-Eurostat_Urban_zones-213">[190]</a></sup></p>
<div style="clear:both;"></div>
